<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Eight high-quality genomes reveal pan-genome architecture and ecotype differentiation of Brassica napus"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2020 6:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-01-13"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Rapeseed (Brassica napus) is the second most important oilseed crop in the world but the genetic diversity underlying its massive phenotypic variations remains largely unexplored. Here, we report the sequencing, de novo assembly and annotation of eight B. napus accessions. Using pan-genome comparative analysis, millions of small variations and 77.2&#8211;149.6&#8201;megabase presence and absence variations (PAVs) were identified. More than 9.4% of the genes contained large-effect mutations or structural variations. PAV-based genome-wide association study (PAV-GWAS) directly identified causal structural variations for silique length, seed weight and flowering time in a nested association mapping population with ZS11 (reference line) as the donor, which were not detected by single-nucleotide polymorphisms-based GWAS (SNP-GWAS), demonstrating that PAV-GWAS was complementary to SNP-GWAS in identifying associations to traits. Further analysis showed that PAVs in three FLOWERING LOCUS C genes were closely related to flowering time and ecotype differentiation. This study provides resources to support a better understanding of the genome architecture and acceleration of the genetic improvement of B. napus. The assembly of eight high-quality rapeseed genomes allows identification of presence and absence variations (PAVs) and small variations. PAV-based genome-wide association analysis uncovered causal variations for agronomic traits and ecotype differentiation."/>

                    </ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Rapeseed (<i>Brassica napus</i>) is the second most important oilseed crop in the world but the genetic diversity underlying its massive phenotypic variations remains largely unexplored. Here, we report the sequencing, de novo assembly and annotation of eight <i>B. napus</i> accessions. Using pan-genome comparative analysis, millions of small variations and 77.2–149.6 megabase presence and absence variations (PAVs) were identified. More than 9.4% of the genes contained large-effect mutations or structural variations. PAV-based genome-wide association study (PAV-GWAS) directly identified causal structural variations for silique length, seed weight and flowering time in a nested association mapping population with ZS11 (reference line) as the donor, which were not detected by single-nucleotide polymorphisms-based GWAS (SNP-GWAS), demonstrating that PAV-GWAS was complementary to SNP-GWAS in identifying associations to traits. Further analysis showed that PAVs in three <i>FLOWERING LOCUS C</i> genes were closely related to flowering time and ecotype differentiation. This study provides resources to support a better understanding of the genome architecture and acceleration of the genetic improvement of <i>B. napus</i>.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The species <i>Brassica napus</i> (AACC, 2<i>n</i> = 38) is an economically important oilseed crop that provides approximately 13–16% of vegetable oil globally<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wang, B. et al. Dissection of the genetic architecture of three seed-quality traits and consequences for breeding in Brassica napus. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1336–1348 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d7326e553">1</a></sup>. <i>B. napus</i> originated in the Mediterranean region about 7,500 years ago by natural hybridization between two diploid progenitors, <i>B. rapa</i> (AA, 2<i>n</i> = 20) and <i>B. oleracea</i> (CC, 2<i>n</i> = 18)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d7326e573">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Lu, K. et al. Whole-genome resequencing reveals Brassica napus origin and genetic loci involved in its improvement. Nat. Commun. 10, 1154 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d7326e576">3</a></sup>. The genetic pool of <i>B. napus</i> has been broadened by the introgression of genes from <i>B. rapa</i> and synthetic materials produced by artificial crossing between the two diploid progenitors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Sun, F. et al. The high-quality genome of Brassica napus cultivar ‘ZS11’ reveals the introgression history in semi-winter morphotype. Plant J. 92, 452–468 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d7326e587">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zou, J. et al. Genome-wide selection footprints and deleterious variations in young Asian allotetraploid rapeseed. Plant Biotechnol. J. 17, 1998–2010 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d7326e590">5</a></sup>. Driven by responses to seasonal changes, such as vernalization requirements, winter hardiness and photoperiod-responsive flowering, <i>B. napus</i> has been domesticated with various characteristics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d7326e597">2</a></sup>. Currently, three ecotypes of <i>B. napus</i> are widely distributed in Europe, Asia, Australia and North America<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zou, J. et al. Genome-wide selection footprints and deleterious variations in young Asian allotetraploid rapeseed. Plant Biotechnol. J. 17, 1998–2010 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d7326e604">5</a></sup>. Winter-type oilseed rapes (WORs) were first cultivated in Europe and have been suggested to be the original form of <i>B. napus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Lu, K. et al. Whole-genome resequencing reveals Brassica napus origin and genetic loci involved in its improvement. Nat. Commun. 10, 1154 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d7326e611">3</a></sup>. After being introduced into China, Australia and North America in the twentieth century, cultivated <i>B. napus</i> has undergone adaptive changes under a combination of natural and artificial selection to suit different geographical environments and climates. Two additional ecotypes, namely, semi-winter oilseed rapes (SWORs) and spring-type oilseed rapes (SORs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Qian, W. et al. Heterotic patterns in rapeseed (Brassica napus L.): I. Crosses between spring and Chinese semi-winter lines. Theor. Appl. Genet. 115, 27–34 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d7326e618">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Qian, W. et al. Introgression of genomic components from Chinese Brassica rapa contributes to widening the genetic diversity in rapeseed (B. napus L.), with emphasis on the evolution of Chinese rapeseed. Theor. Appl. Genet. 113, 49–54 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d7326e621">7</a></sup>, adapted to different vernalization times and temperatures, were gradually formed.</p><p><i>B. napus</i> is an allotetraploid crop with a complex genome; there are extensive genomic and phenotypic variations in different accessions and ecotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d7326e630">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zou, J. et al. Genome-wide selection footprints and deleterious variations in young Asian allotetraploid rapeseed. Plant Biotechnol. J. 17, 1998–2010 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d7326e633">5</a></sup>. The genomes of several rapeseed accessions, including two WORs (Darmor-<i>bzh</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d7326e639">2</a></sup> and Tapidor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bayer, P. E. et al. Assembly and comparison of two closely related Brassica napus genomes. Plant Biotechnol. J. 15, 1602–1610 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d7326e643">8</a></sup>) and two SWORs (ZS11, ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Sun, F. et al. The high-quality genome of Brassica napus cultivar ‘ZS11’ reveals the introgression history in semi-winter morphotype. Plant J. 92, 452–468 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d7326e647">4</a></sup> and NY7, ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zou, J. et al. Genome-wide selection footprints and deleterious variations in young Asian allotetraploid rapeseed. Plant Biotechnol. J. 17, 1998–2010 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d7326e652">5</a></sup>), have been decoded, providing useful resources for genetic studies such as gene mapping and cloning. However, the current <i>B. napus</i> genomes were primarily assembled on the basis of 454 GS-FLX + Titanium and Sanger sequence, next-generation sequencing (NGS) data or medium-coverage PacBio single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing data. Their accuracy and completeness are unsatisfactory for identifying structural variations (SVs), which are major contributors to genetic diversity and play key roles in the determination of agronomic traits in many crop species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045–1052 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d7326e659">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Tao, Y., Zhao, X., Mace, E., Henry, R. &amp; Jordan, D. Exploring and exploiting pan-genomics for crop improvement. Mol. Plant 12, 156–169 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d7326e662">10</a></sup>. The concept of the pan-genome was proposed to represent a repertoire of genes including the core genes and dispensable genes of a species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Tettelin, H. et al. Genome analysis of multiple pathogenic isolates of Streptococcus agalactiae: implications for the microbial “pan-genome”. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 13950–13955 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d7326e666">11</a></sup>. Pan-genomes have been constructed on the basis of NGS technologies for major crops, including soybean, maize, rapeseed and rice, using different numbers of individuals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045–1052 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d7326e670">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lu, F. et al. High-resolution genetic mapping of maize pan-genome sequence anchors. Nat. Commun. 6, 6914 (2015)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d7326e673">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hurgobin, B. et al. Homoeologous exchange is a major cause of gene presence/absence variation in the amphidiploid Brassica napus. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1265–1274 (2018)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d7326e673_1">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zhao, Q. et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat. Genet. 50, 278–284 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d7326e676">14</a></sup>. These pan-genomes play important roles in the identification of SVs, including copy number variants (CNVs) and presence and absence variations (PAVs) that are associated with crop agronomic traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Tao, Y., Zhao, X., Mace, E., Henry, R. &amp; Jordan, D. Exploring and exploiting pan-genomics for crop improvement. Mol. Plant 12, 156–169 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d7326e680">10</a></sup>.</p><p>Although several genomes are available for <i>B. napus</i>, they cannot represent these genetic variations or satisfy the needs of subsequent functional genomics research and molecular breeding of <i>B. napus</i>. Multiple high-quality reference genomes representing different ecotypes are necessary for a better understanding of the genome structure and genetic basis of morphotype differentiation in <i>B. napus</i>. In this study, ZS11 de novo assemblies were created by integrating PacBio, Hi-C and BioNano data; the other seven accessions were obtained by integrating high-coverage PacBio and Illumina data; two of them were verified by Hi-C or BioNano data. We performed a genome-wide comparative analysis of these eight well-assembled genomes and the Darmor-<i>bzh</i> genome and identified the core-gene clusters, dispensable gene clusters and specific gene clusters. As a proof of concept for the importance of the pan-genome, we identified the causal PAVs that control silique length, seed weight and flowering time of oilseed rape.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">De novo assembly and annotation of eight <i>B. napus</i> genomes</h3><p>Eight oilseed rape lines, including four SWORs (ZS11, Gangan, Zheyou7 and Shengli), two WORs (Tapidor and Quinta) and two SORs (Westar and No2127, ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Chen, B. Y., Heneen, W. K. &amp; Jonsson, R. Resynthesis of Brassica napus L. through interspecific hybridization between B. alboglabra Bailey and B. campestris L. with special emphasis on seed colour. Plant Breed. 101, 52–59 (1988)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d7326e715">15</a></sup>, a synthetic line), were sequenced with the PacBio SMRT platform (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">1</a>). We generated 64.5–97.1 gigabase (Gb) subreads for the eight lines, with an estimated coverage depth of 64−96X for the different genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">2</a>) and the assembled contig N50 was in the range of 2.1−3.1 megabases (Mb) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">3</a>). The contigs of ZS11 were corrected, ordered and oriented using Hi-C data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289–293 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d7326e732">16</a></sup> and clustered into 19 chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig1">1a,b</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">2</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">4</a>). The other accessions were oriented on the basis of the ZS11 reference genome (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Sec10">Methods</a>). The different chromosome size of eight <i>B. napus</i> genomes might be caused by homologous exchange (HE) events (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">3</a>). Our <i>k</i>-mer analysis suggested a genome size of 1,200–1,280 Mb for each genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">5</a>), which is close to the estimated genome size of <i>B. napus</i> (~1,132 Mb) according to flow cytometry analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Johnston, J. S. et al. Evolution of genome size in Brassicaceae. Ann. Bot. 95, 229–235 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d7326e764">17</a></sup>. Finally, we obtained eight <i>B. napus</i> reference genomes with scaffolds N50 of 50.90−57.88 Mb and genome lengths of 1,001−1,033 Mb. For the ZS11 reference genome, 960.8 Mb (95.3%) of sequence was anchored to 19 chromosomes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">6</a>). These indicator values are greatly improved compared with those of previously <i>B. napus</i> genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d7326e781">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Sun, F. et al. The high-quality genome of Brassica napus cultivar ‘ZS11’ reveals the introgression history in semi-winter morphotype. Plant J. 92, 452–468 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d7326e784">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zou, J. et al. Genome-wide selection footprints and deleterious variations in young Asian allotetraploid rapeseed. Plant Biotechnol. J. 17, 1998–2010 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d7326e787">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bayer, P. E. et al. Assembly and comparison of two closely related Brassica napus genomes. Plant Biotechnol. J. 15, 1602–1610 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d7326e790">8</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Features of the B. napus genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Features of the <i>B. napus</i> genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0577-7/MediaObjects/41477_2019_577_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0577-7/MediaObjects/41477_2019_577_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="439" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Circos plot of the multidimensional topography for <i>B. napus</i> ZS11 genome. A–H, Concentric circles from outermost to innermost, show GC content (A), gene density (B), TE density (C), A/B compartment (D), SNP density in No2127 (E), SNP density in Tapidor (F), SNP density in Shengli (G) and syntenic regions between the A and C subgenomes (H). <b>b</b>, Genome-wide contact matrix of ZS11 genome. The colour intensity represents the frequency of contact between two 500 kb loci. <b>c</b>, Interaction frequency, A/B compartment and gene density in ZS11 chromosome A01. The colour scale represents the Pearson’s correlation coefficient of normalized interaction matrix. Eigv, eigenvector value of correlation matrix.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of genomic assembly and annotation for eight <i>B. napus</i> genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The completeness of all eight assembled genomes exceeded 98% (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">7</a>) when evaluated using BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Simão, F. A. et al. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d7326e978">18</a></sup> and CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061–1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d7326e982">19</a></sup>. The centromere sequences were successfully identified for all chromosomes of ZS11 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">8</a>). The accuracy and completeness of the assembly were further supported by high matches with 11 BAC sequences, paired-end short reads of 13 RNA-seq libraries and Illumina genomic paired-end short reads of each accession (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">9</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">11</a>). We aligned 8,858 and 5,722 paired BAC-end sequences (BESs) from the ZS11 and Tapidor BAC libraries, respectively, to the assembled ZS11 genome. The average distances between paired BESs were within the range of the estimated insertion sizes, suggesting the high-quality of the genome assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">12</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">4</a>). The genetic map with 7,158 single-nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) markers derived from the cross of <i>B. napus</i> ZS11 and Quantum showed a good collinearity with the physical map of ZS11 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">5</a>). In addition, the pseudo-chromosomes of No2127 generated by Hi-C data showed good synteny with those based on the ZS11 genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">6</a>). The scaffolds assembled by BioNano optical maps for No2127 and Westar covered ~66% of the reference genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">13</a>) and were highly consistent with the 19 chromosomes, indicating the accuracy of contig orientation (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">8</a>). Only 86 and 99 conflicts were identified between the assembled genomes and BioNano optical maps for No2127 and Westar, respectively (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">15</a>).</p><p>The genome assemblies are highly improved in regions containing highly repetitive sequences. In total, 56.8−58.2% transposable elements (TEs) were identified in the eight assembled genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig1">1a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">16</a>), which is much higher than the proportion in the published Darmor-<i>bzh</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1039">2</a></sup> (34.8%), further supporting the completeness of the genome assembly. Long terminal repeat (LTR)/Gypsy repeat elements were the most abundant, accounting for ~16% of the genome, followed by LTR/Copia. Overall, the proportion of retrotransposons (RTs, ~36%) was much higher than that of DNA transposons (~10%). We identified 24,704 intact LTR-RTs from the ZS11 genome and estimated their insertion times (substitution rate 1.5 × 10<sup>−8</sup>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Koch, M. A., Haubold, B. &amp; Mitchell-Olds, T. Comparative evolutionary analysis of chalcone synthase and alcohol dehydrogenase loci in Arabidopsis, Arabis, and related genera (Brassicaceae). Mol. Biol. Evol. 17, 1483–1498 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1046">20</a></sup>. The insertions of 93% of the intact LTR-RTs occurred after the differentiation of <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i>. The intact LTR-RTs in the C subgenome continuously amplified over 3 Myr since the differentiation of <i>B. oleracea</i> and <i>B. rapa</i>, resulting in a wide distribution of LTR-RTs in euchromatic regions and a larger genome size than the A subgenome. However, the A subgenome had expanded within the last 1 Myr (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">9</a>), similar to the expansion observed in the genomes of its diploid progenitors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Zhao, M. et al. Shifts in the evolutionary rate and intensity of purifying selection between two Brassica genomes revealed by analyses of orthologous transposons and relics of a whole genome triplication. Plant J. 76, 211–222 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1066">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Liu, S. et al. The Brassica oleracea genome reveals the asymmetrical evolution of polyploid genomes. Nat. Commun. 5, 3930 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1069">22</a></sup>, indicating that there was a higher proportion of active LTR-RTs in the A subgenome.</p><p>The remaining unmasked <i>B. napus</i> genome was annotated using a comprehensive strategy combining evidence-based and ab initio gene prediction. Using PASA and the EVM pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1079">23</a></sup>, we incorporated protein sequences from six related species and the transcripts assembled from the RNA-seq data of each accession. We identified 94,586–100,919 gene models in the eight genomes, with an average coding sequence length of ~1 kilobase (kb) and an average of five exons per gene (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">17</a>). More than 97% of the annotated genes are supported by homology to known proteins or functional domains in other species (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">18</a>). On a genome-wide scale, protein-coding genes tend to be distributed in chromosome arms with lower GC content and fewer repeat elements (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig1">1a</a>). In addition, 21,430−27,390 non-coding RNAs, including ribosomal RNAs (rRNAs), transfer RNAs (tRNAs), microRNAs (miRNAs) and small nuclear RNAs (snRNAs), were annotated in each of the eight <i>B. napus</i> genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">19</a>).</p><p>As shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig1">1b</a>, the Hi-C data of all the ZS11 chromosomes showed a strong signal on the main diagonal, indicating frequent interactions between adjacent loci. Strong intrachromosomal interactions were also observed between chromosome arms, which were consistent with the chromosome concept, in which each chromosome occupies a limited, exclusive nuclear space<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Tiang, C. L., He, Y. &amp; Pawlowski, W. P. Chromosome organization and dynamics during interphase, mitosis, and meiosis in plants. Plant Physiol. 158, 26–34 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1106">24</a></sup>. The Hi-C map of <i>B. napus</i> showed similar A/B compartment characteristics to mammal genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289–293 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1113">16</a></sup>, where the B compartment is concentrated in the centromere region, with higher transposon density and the A compartment is mainly distributed on the chromosome arm with higher gene density (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig1">1c</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Phylogenetic analysis of <i>B. napus</i> and other <i>Brassica</i> species</h3><p><i>Brassica</i> has undergone lineage-specific whole-genome triplication and the <i>B. napus</i> genome was formed via allotetraploidization of its diploid progenitors <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">10a</a>). In the ZS11 genome, up to six homologous copies can be found in a single synteny block, which indicated traces of paralogue retention following triploidization and allotetraploidization (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">10b</a>). On the basis of the coding sequences of 1,235 single-copy orthologous genes, we constructed a <i>Brassica</i> phylogeny for the 20 subgenomes/genomes with <i>A. thaliana</i> as the outgroup. The structure of the phylogenetic tree clearly indicates that two subgenomes of the resynthesized allotetraploid No2127 are the closest to the two ancestral genomes of <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> and rapeseed lines of the same <i>B. napus</i> ecotypes clustered together (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig2">2a</a>). Synonymous substitution rate (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) analysis indicated that <i>B. napus</i> differentiated from its diploid progenitors, <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i>, ~10,000 years ago (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">11</a>). <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> differentiated ~3 million years ago (Ma), which is close to a previous estimation based on SLR1 genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Inaba, R. &amp; Nishio, T. Phylogenetic analysis of Brassiceae based on the nucleotide sequences of the S‐locus related gene, SLR1. Theor. Appl. Genet. 105, 1159–1165 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1198">25</a></sup>. A whole-genome triplication of <i>Brassica</i> species occurred ~11 Ma and <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>B. napus</i> differentiated ~14 Ma. The triplicated regions of the 19 chromosomes of ZS11 were reconstructed according to the relative positions of the 22 ancestral crucifer karyotype blocks (A–X) in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Lysak, M. A., Mandakova, T. &amp; Schranz, M. E. Comparative paleogenomics of crucifers: ancestral genomic blocks revisited. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 30, 108–115 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1214">26</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">12</a>). These syntenic blocks occupy almost the whole-genome assemblies of the A (43,966 of 44,359 genes) and C subgenomes (52,067 of 52,562 genes) in ZS11 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">20</a>). On the basis of the high-quality ZS11 reference genome, we identified 297 MADS-box genes, which is more than previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Duan, W. et al. Genome-wide analysis of the MADS-box gene family in Brassica rapa (Chinese cabbage). Mol. Genet. Genomics 290, 239–255 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1224">27</a></sup>. Some MADS-box genes have been shown to be targets of domestication and variety improvement<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zhao, Q., Weber, A. L., McMullen, M. D., Guill, K. &amp; Doebley, J. MADS-box genes of maize: frequent targets of selection during domestication. Genet. Res. 93, 65–75 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1229">28</a></sup>. We identified 28 MADS-box genes that had undergone positive selection in the ZS11 genome, including <i>BnaA10.FLC</i>, which is closely related to flowering time and reproductive development (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">13</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">21</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Phylogenetic analysis of Brassicaceae."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Phylogenetic analysis of <i>Brassicaceae</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0577-7/MediaObjects/41477_2019_577_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0577-7/MediaObjects/41477_2019_577_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="385" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Phylogenetic relationship of nine <i>B. napus</i> genomes and their diploid progenitors, <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i>. The phylogenetic tree is constructed on the basis of 1,235 conserved genes. The values on the branch are the substitutions between species and the nearest ancestor. WGT, whole genome triplication. <b>b</b>, A neighbour-joining tree of 210 <i>B. napus</i> accessions, eight assembled accessions and 199 <i>B. rapa</i> accessions. Each assembled accession was represented by a pentagram (left to right: Westar, Quinta, Tapidor, Shengli, Zheyou, Gangan, ZS11 and No2127). The layer rings indicate the group name of each clade. <b>c</b>, PCA plot of <i>B. napus</i> (<i>n</i> = 210) and <i>B. rapa</i> (<i>n</i> = 199) accessions. <b>d</b>, PCA plot of <i>B. napus</i> (<i>n</i> = 210) and <i>B. oleracea</i> (<i>n</i> = 119) accessions.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To study the origins of the <i>B. napus</i> subgenomes, we analysed SNPs in the A subgenomes of 210 <i>B. napus</i> accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wang, B. et al. Dissection of the genetic architecture of three seed-quality traits and consequences for breeding in Brassica napus. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1336–1348 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1316">1</a></sup>, the eight accessions assembled in this study and 199 <i>B. rapa</i> accessions covering most subspecies of <i>B. rapa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Cheng, F. et al. Sub-genome parallel selection is associated with morphotype diversification and convergent crop domestication in Brassica rapa and Brassica oleracea. Nat. Genet. 48, 1218–1224 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1325">29</a></sup>. We constructed a neighbour-joining tree for the A subgenomes of <i>B. napus</i> and found that all the A subgenomes were rooted in the common ancestor turnip (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig2">2b</a>), indicating that the A subgenomes may derive from turnip, in agreement with a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Yang, J. et al. The genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and analysis of differential homoeolog gene expression influencing selection. Nat. Genet. 48, 1225–1232 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1336">30</a></sup>. Principal component analysis (PCA) also located <i>B. napus</i> close to turnip accessions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig2">2c</a>), supporting the results of the phylogenetic tree. The 210 <i>B. napus</i> accessions included SWORs from China and WORs and SORs from Europe, Canada, Australia and other countries. SWORs were closer to turnip than to SORs and WORs in the phylogenetic tree. PCA also showed that SWORs were close to turnip (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig2">2c</a>).</p><p>Similarly, a neighbour-joining tree of the 210 <i>B. napus</i> and 119 <i>B. oleracea</i> accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Cheng, F. et al. Sub-genome parallel selection is associated with morphotype diversification and convergent crop domestication in Brassica rapa and Brassica oleracea. Nat. Genet. 48, 1218–1224 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1362">29</a></sup> was constructed using tag SNPs in the C subgenome. However, the origin of the C subgenome in <i>B. napus</i> was not robustly inferred (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig2">2d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">14</a>), which is consistent with previous results that the C subgenome in <i>B. napus</i> originated during the divergence of the ancestors of four <i>B. oleracea</i> subspecies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Lu, K. et al. Whole-genome resequencing reveals Brassica napus origin and genetic loci involved in its improvement. Nat. Commun. 10, 1154 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1382">3</a></sup>. The 210 <i>B. napus</i> lines were classified into different subclades that roughly corresponded to three ecotypes. Of the eight sequenced rapeseed lines, ZS11, Gangan, Shengli and Zheyou7 were clustered with the SWORs, Quinta and Tapidor with the WORs, Westar with the SORs and the artificial synthesized line No2127 with the unknown type. The node positions of the eight reference accessions in the phylogenetic tree of the <i>B. napus</i> A subgenome were similar to those in the C subgenome tree. This result illustrated that the genetic diversity of these eight accessions had good typicality or representativeness of natural rapeseed populations.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Large genomic variations in eight genomes of <i>B. napus</i>
</h3><p>To compare the genomic differences among the eight assembled <i>B. napus</i> genomes, we aligned the other seven genomes to ZS11. In general, 76−84% of the ZS11 genome sequences were in one-to-one syntenic blocks to the other seven genomes, with the lowest ratio found in the resynthesized line No2127 (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">16</a>). In the aligned syntenic regions, 1.87−3.93 × 10<sup>6</sup> high-quality SNPs and 0.98−1.48 × 10<sup>6</sup> small insertions/deletions (InDels) were identified (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">22</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">23</a>), with an average of 2.24−5.18 SNPs and 1.17−1.95 InDels per kb in different <i>B. napus</i> genomes. The distributions of SNPs and InDels were positively correlated (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">17</a>) and both were more abundant in intergenic regions. The number of synonymous SNPs was much larger than that of non-synonymous SNPs and InDels were more abundant with multiples of 3 bp in coding regions, as expected, because such InDels do not cause frameshifts (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">18</a>).</p><p>The high-quality reference genomes allowed us to identify large SVs by comparative genomic analysis between different accessions. Compared with the ZS11 genome, 7.5−15.6 Mb were identified as inversions in each of the other seven accessions, with 40 large inversions of &gt;50 kb (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">19</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">24</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">25</a>). We also detected 39.7−49.1 Mb translocations, with more interchromosomal translocations (2,343−5,957) than intrachromosomal translocations (1,157−2,149). Most of the translocations were caused by HE events (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">26</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">27</a>). We further characterized PAVs by comparing the ZS11 reference genome with the other seven genomes. In total, 16,720−34,158 regions with cumulative lengths of 77.2−149.6 Mb were identified as absent in the ZS11 genome and these regions were associated with 2,619−4,810 genes in the other seven genomes (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">28</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">35</a>). Gene ontology enrichment analysis showed that the genes in PAV regions were enriched in defence response, signal transduction, response to stress and so on (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">36</a>). In addition to the large SVs, 9.4−14.9% of the genes contained large-effect mutations, including frameshifts, gain or loss of stop codons, or other variations causing major protein differences (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">37</a>). Consequently, extensive intraspecific genomic variations exist and may cause massive phenotypic variations in <i>B. napus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Harper, A. L. et al. Associative transcriptomics of traits in the polyploid crop species Brassica napus. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 798 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1466">31</a></sup>. The eight high-quality reference genomes provide important resources for capturing the full landscape of genetic diversity in different <i>B. napus</i> genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Pan-genome and gene index of <i>B. napus</i>
</h3><p>Referring to the pan-genome definition in corn<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Hirsch, C. N. et al. Insights into the maize pan-genome and pan-transcriptome. Plant Cell 26, 121–135 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1486">32</a></sup> and <i>B. oleracea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Golicz, A. A. et al. The pangenome of an agronomically important crop plant Brassica oleracea. Nat. Commun. 7, 13390 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1492">33</a></sup>, we constructed the pan-genome of <i>B. napus</i> by adding the PAV sequences from the individual genomes to the ZS11 genome and obtained a pan-reference genome with a genome size of ~1.8 Gb and 152,185 genes. The genome size and gene number increased as the number of genomes increased but the number of orthologous gene clusters did not increase after combining six genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">20</a>), indicating that the gene families in the <i>B. napus</i> pan-reference genome tend to be saturated when integrating six genomes representing the three ecotypes. We further analysed the following four conceptual groups of genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Vesth, T. C. et al. Investigation of inter- and intraspecies variation through genome sequencing of Aspergillus section Nigri. Nat. Genet. 50, 1688–1695 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1506">34</a></sup> in nine genomes, including our eight genomes and the Darmor-<i>bzh</i> genome: the pan-gene clusters (the cumulative set of all genes), the core-gene clusters (genes present in <i>n</i> ≥ 7 genomes containing paralogues), the dispensable gene clusters (genes present in 2−6 genomes containing paralogues) and the specific gene clusters (present in only one genome, including specific gene clusters and singletons). Orthologous and paralogous genes were identified on the basis of OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1516">35</a></sup>. All 874,105 annotated genes in the nine <i>B. napus</i> genomes were assigned to 105,672 gene clusters (group of homologous genes) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">38</a>). Of these gene clusters, 58,714 (~56%) were core-gene clusters that existed in at least seven genomes and 44,035 (~42%) were dispensable gene clusters. The remaining ~2% (2,923 gene clusters and 5,041 singletons) were specific genes with no orthologues in other genomes. We then performed gene ontogeny enrichment analysis to associate biological functions with the pan-, core- and specific-genes. Generally, basic functions, such as ‘regulation of biosynthetic process’, ‘transport and primary metabolic process’, were enriched in the core-gene clusters of <i>B. napus</i> and 86% of the genes in the core genome contained known functional domains. On the other hand, ‘response to stimulation’ or ‘stress and protein phosphorylation’ was enriched in the specific gene clusters of almost all accessions (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">21</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">27</a>). Secondary metabolism and biological process regulatory proteins were often identified as specific genes and thus represent critical genetic differences in <i>B. napus</i>. For convenience of gene comparison among different rapeseed lines and retrieval of genes of interest, we constructed a unique gene index in the above nine <i>B. napus</i> genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">39</a>). Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig3">3b</a> shows an example of the gene index HUBnaA01G0071 in ZS11 and the other eight genomes. The exon–intron structure of HUBnaA01G0071 is conserved in all nine genomes. In the gene indices, 35.5−55.9% of ZS11 genes are identical to genes in the other eight genomes, while 36.7−58.3%, 1.2−2.1% and &lt;0.06% have high identity (&gt;80%), medium identity (50−80%) and low identity (&lt;50%) to genes in the other eight genomes, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">40</a>). A pan-reference genome coordinate system was constructed (<a href="http://cbi.hzau.edu.cn/bnapus">http://cbi.hzau.edu.cn/bnapus</a>) for quick querying, viewing and downloading gene indexes, gene annotations and SVs across different <i>B. napus</i> genomes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="The pan-genome and gene index of nine B. napus accessions."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: The pan-genome and gene index of nine <i>B. napus</i> accessions.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0577-7/MediaObjects/41477_2019_577_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0577-7/MediaObjects/41477_2019_577_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="327" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Core- and pan-genome of <i>B. napus</i>. The upper circle diagram shows the ratio of homologous genes to orphan genes and the table lists the detailed number. The histograms below show the core-gene clusters (present in seven or more genomes), dispensable gene clusters (present in two to six genomes) and specific gene clusters (present in one genome). <b>b</b>, An example of <i>B. napus</i> Gene Index. HUBnaA01G007100 is the unique gene ID of A01 <i>MAPKK2</i> gene across nine <i>B. napus</i> genomes. The axis is the physical location of the gene in the ZS11 genome. The blue column is an accumulation of multi-tissue RNA-seq reads map. Grey blocks are collinearly aligned regions. Annotated gene structure in each genome is in the black box.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">SNP-based GWAS versus PAV-based GWAS: case studies for silique length, seed weight and flowering time</h3><p>Many studies show that large SVs have a more significant influence on traits than SNPs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang, N. et al. Genome assembly of a tropical maize inbred line provides insights into structural variation and crop improvement. Nat. Genet. 51, 1052–1059 (2019)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1598">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gaut, B. S., Seymour, D. K., Liu, Q. &amp; Zhou, Y. Demography and its effects on genomic variation in crop domestication. Nat. Plants 4, 512–520 (2018)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1598_1">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Lye, Z. N. &amp; Purugganan, M. D. Copy number variation in domestication. Trends Plant Sci. 24, 352–365 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1601">38</a></sup>, while traditional SNP-based genome-wide association studies (SNP-GWASs) have difficulty in detecting missing content in the reference genome. PAVs have been reported as the causal variations of several important traits in <i>B. napus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Hurgobin, B. et al. Homoeologous exchange is a major cause of gene presence/absence variation in the amphidiploid Brassica napus. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1265–1274 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1607">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Liu, J. et al. Natural variation in ARF18 gene simultaneously affects seed weight and silique length in polyploid rapeseed. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, E5123–E5132 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1610">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Li, S. P. et al. BnaC9.SMG7b functions as a positive regulator of the number of seeds per silique in Brassica napus by regulating the formation of functional female gametophytes. Plant Physiol. 169, 2744–2760 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1613">40</a></sup>. To explore the contribution of SVs to trait variation, we conducted GWAS on three important yield-related traits, including silique length (SL), seed weight (SW) and flowering time. This was done on the basis of 3,971,412 SNPs and 27,216 PAVs (PAV-GWAS) with minor allele frequencies (MAFs) higher than 0.05 in a <i>B. napus</i> nested association mapping (NAM) population. The NAM population was developed by crossing ZS11 with 15 diverse founder inbred lines, including two reference inbred lines, Zheyou7 and Gangan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Hu, J. et al. Genetic properties of a nested association mapping population constructed with semi-winter and spring oilseed rapes. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 1740 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1620">41</a></sup> (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">41</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">43</a>). The PAV genotypes of the 16 NAM parental lines were identified by BayesTyper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Sibbesen, J. A., Maretty, L., Danish Pan-Genome Consortium. &amp; Krogh, A. Accurate genotyping across variant classes and lengths using variant graphs. Nat. Genet. 50, 1054–1059 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1631">42</a></sup> and projected onto the 2,141 recombinant inbred lines (RILs) on the basis of the high-density genetic linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Hu, J. et al. Genetic properties of a nested association mapping population constructed with semi-winter and spring oilseed rapes. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 1740 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1635">41</a></sup>. SNP-GWAS identified 75 and 38 SNPs associated with SL and SW, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">44</a>). Although the peak SNP on chromosome A09 fell within the previously reported region identified by traditional quantitative trait locus mapping and positional cloning<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Yang, P. Identification of a major QTL for silique length and seed weight in oilseed rape (Brassica napus L.). Theor. Appl. Genet. 125, 285–296 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1642">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Shi, L. et al. A CACTA‐like transposable element in the upstream region of BnaA9. CYP 78A9 acts as an enhancer to increase silique length and seed weight in rapeseed. Plant J. 98, 524–539 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1645">44</a></sup>, none of the associated SNPs was located in the regulatory region or coding sequence of the target gene <i>BnaA9.CYP78A9</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig4">4a,b</a>). Encouragingly, PAV-GWAS directly detected the 3.9-kb CACTA-like TE inserted upstream of the <i>BnaA9.CYP78A9</i> promoter region, which was identified as the causal variation for SL and SW<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Shi, L. et al. A CACTA‐like transposable element in the upstream region of BnaA9. CYP 78A9 acts as an enhancer to increase silique length and seed weight in rapeseed. Plant J. 98, 524–539 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1659">44</a></sup> (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">28</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">29</a>). The NAM individuals containing the CACTA-like TE insertion had longer siliques and larger seeds than those not containing the TE insertion (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig4">4c–h</a>). Previous studies have demonstrated that the inserted TE acts as an enhancer, promoting the high expression of <i>BnaA9.CYP78A9</i> preferentially in the silique valves of varieties with long siliques and large seeds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Shi, L. et al. A CACTA‐like transposable element in the upstream region of BnaA9. CYP 78A9 acts as an enhancer to increase silique length and seed weight in rapeseed. Plant J. 98, 524–539 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1676">44</a></sup>. Among the eight genomes, Gangan and ZS11 had TE insertions upstream of the <i>BnaA9.CYP78A9</i> promoter. Consequently, these two lines showed significantly greater SL and higher SW than the others (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig4">4b,e–h</a>). This result indicates that PAV-GWAS is complementary to SNP-GWAS in identifying associations with phenotypes caused by SVs.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="GWAS of silique length and seed weight in the NAM population."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: GWAS of silique length and seed weight in the NAM population.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0577-7/MediaObjects/41477_2019_577_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0577-7/MediaObjects/41477_2019_577_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="473" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Manhattan plots of SNP-GWAS and PAV-GWAS for silique length. <b>b</b>, A 3.6-kb CACTA-like insertion as lead PAV of <i>BnaA09.CYP78A9</i> promoter region. <b>c</b>, The silique length in lines with different <i>CYP78A9</i> alleles. For <b>a</b> and <b>b</b>, the GWAS (-lmmm 1: Wald test) was performed with 3,971,412 SNPs or 27,216 PAVs in the BN-NAM population containing 2,141 RILs. <b>d</b>, Thousand-seed weight in lines with different <i>CYP78A9</i> alleles. For <b>c</b> and <b>d</b>, <i>P</i> values were determined using two-tailed Student’s <i>t</i>-tests. The middle bars represent the median while the bottom and top of each box represent the 25th and 75th percentiles, respectively. The whiskers extend to 1.5 times the interquartile range. Alt, alternative; Ref, reference. <b>e</b>,<b>f</b>, Phenotype data of silique length in eight <i>B. napus</i> accessions. <b>g</b>,<b>h</b>, Phenotype data of seed weight in eight assembled <i>B. napus</i> accessions. For <b>e</b> and <b>g</b>, experiments were repeated five times with similar results. For <b>f</b> and <b>h</b>, data are mean ± s.d. of eight and five biological replicates, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Flowering time is a complex agronomic and quantitative trait reflecting the adaptation of <i>B. napus</i> to its environment by tailoring vegetative and reproductive growth phases to climatic effects. To dissect the genetic architecture of flowering time, we grew the NAM population in multiple environments, including two spring environments and six winter environments. The flowering time (days from sowing to flowering) showed extensive variation among different RILs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">45</a>). SNP-GWAS identified 63 and 79 loci associated with flowering time in the winter and spring environments, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig5">5a,b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">46</a>). Most of the loci identified in the spring environment overlapped with those identified in the winter environment. Underlying these loci, 67 genes orthologous to <i>Arabidopsis</i> genes controlling flowering time were identified, including three <i>FLOWERING LOCUS C</i> (<i>FLC</i>), two <i>FLOWERING LOCUS T</i> (<i>FT</i>), two <i>VERNALIZATION INSENSITIVE 3</i> (<i>VIN3</i>) and two <i>CRYPTOCHROME 2</i> (<i>CRY2</i>) genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig5">5a,b</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">46</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">47</a>). PAV-GWAS detected only three consistent peaks in both the spring and winter environments. The peak PAVs were directly located within two orthologues of <i>Arabidopsis FLC</i>, <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> and <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig5">5c,d</a>). The peak PAV (824 bp) on A02 is a partial sequence of hAT retrotransposon located in the sixth exon of <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig5">5h</a>). NAM RILs with this 824-bp PAV flowered much earlier than those without it in both the winter and spring environments (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig5">5i,j</a>). The peak PAV on A10 was a 4,421-bp hAT inserted in the promoter region of <i>BnaA10.FLC</i>, which was not reported in a previous GWAS studies with nearly 1,000 re-sequenced rapeseed accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Wu, D. et al. Whole-genome resequencing of a worldwide collection of rapeseed accessions reveals the genetic basis of ecotype divergence. Mol. Plant 12, 30–43 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1845">45</a></sup>.
