NAM RILs with this hAT insertion flowered later than those without it in spring environments (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">30</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="GWAS of flowering time in the NAM population."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: GWAS of flowering time in the NAM population.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0577-7/MediaObjects/41477_2019_577_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0577-7/MediaObjects/41477_2019_577_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="680" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>,<b>b</b>, Manhattan plots for flowering time analysed by SNP-GWAS in winter and spring environments, respectively. The gray dashed lines indicate the significance threshold. The BLUP values of the days from sowing to flowering (DTF) in the winter and spring environments were used to represent the flowering time for SNP-GWAS. The triangles and arrows denote the main candidate genes surrounding the strong peaks. <b>c</b>,<b>d</b>, Manhattan plots for flowering time analysed by PAV-GWAS in winter and spring environments, respectively. The BLUP values of DTFs in winter and spring environments were used to represent the flowering time for PAV-GWAS. The gray dashed lines indicate the significance threshold. <b>e</b>–<b>g</b>, Local Manhattan plots, gene positions and LD heatmaps show the regions surrounding the strong peaks of the candidate genes (<i>BnaA02.FLC</i>, <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> and <i>BnaC02.FLC</i>) identified by SNP-GWAS. <b>h</b>, An 824-bp hAT insertion in the last exon of <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> was identified as the lead PAV by PAV-GWAS. For <b>a</b>–<b>h</b>, the GWAS (-lmmm 1: Wald test) was performed with 3,971,412 SNPs or 27,216 PAVs in the BN-NAM population containing 2,141 RILs. <b>i</b>,<b>j</b>, Flowering time of lines with different <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> alleles in spring (<b>i</b>) and winter (<b>j</b>), respectively. <i>P</i> values were determined using two-tailed Student’s <i>t</i>-tests. The middle bars represent the median, while the bottom and top of each box represent the 25th and 75th percentiles, respectively. The whiskers extend to 1.5 times the interquartile range.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">The role of <i>FLC</i> genes in the divergence of the three rapeseed ecotypes</h3><p><i>FLC</i> has been reported as a key transcriptional regulator that delays flowering by repressing the expression of floral integrators such as <i>FT</i>, <i>SUPPRESSOR OF OVEREXPRESSION OF CO1</i> (<i>SOC1</i>) and <i>FD</i> in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Helliwell, C. A., Wood, C. C., Robertson, M., James Peacock, W. &amp; Dennis, E. S. The Arabidopsis FLC protein interacts directly in vivo with SOC1 and FT chromatin and is part of a high-molecular-weight protein complex. Plant J. 46, 183–192 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1952">46</a></sup>. <i>B. napus</i> contains multiple copies of <i>FLC</i> and several homologues are associated with flowering time variation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Tadege, M. et al. Control of flowering time by FLC orthologues in Brassica napus. Plant J. 28, 545–553 (2001)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d7326e1962">47</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig5">5e–g</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">31</a>). We compared the gene structures of <i>BnaA02.FLC</i>, <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> and <i>BnaC02.FLC</i> among our eight assembled <i>B. napus</i> genomes and their diploid progenitors <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i>. Four TEs were identified in the promoter and coding region of <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig6">6a</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">32</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">33</a>). A 5,565-bp LINE transposon was identified only in the first exons of the two SORs (No2127 and Westar), while a 621-bp MITE transposon was identified only in the promoter regions of two WORs (Quinta and Tapidor), although it was also present in the genome of Darmor-<i>bzh</i>. This MITE was previously identified as specific to WORs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Hou, J. et al. A Tourist-like MITE insertion in the upstream region of the BnFLC. A10 gene is associated with vernalization requirement in rapeseed (Brassica napus L.). BMC Plant Biol. 12, 238 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2007">48</a></sup>. The 4,421-bp hAT retrotransposon was identified only in the promoter regions of the four SWORs (ZS11, Zheyou7, Gangan and Shengli). In addition, the 1,656-bp LTR was found in the promoter regions of two (Shengli and Zheyou7) out of the four SWORs. We further validated these TEs in 210 <i>B. napus</i> accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wang, B. et al. Dissection of the genetic architecture of three seed-quality traits and consequences for breeding in Brassica napus. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1336–1348 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2015">1</a></sup> sequenced with an average depth of 7× (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">48</a>), 141 of which had ecotype information<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Yi, L. et al. Sequence variation and functional analysis of a FRIGIDA orthologue (BnaA3. FRI) in Brassica napus. BMC Plant Biol. 18, 32 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2022">49</a></sup>. Five characteristic sequence sites were selected from each of the LINE, MITE and hAT insertions to represent the corresponding TEs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig6">6b</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">34</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">Supplementary Notes</a>). Of the 210 <i>B. napus</i> accessions, 34 contained the MITE insertion, 60 contained the hAT insertion and 129 contained the LINE insertion (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">49</a>). Notably, all the WORs contained the MITE insertion, 85% (22/26) of the SORs contained the LINE insertion and 81% (80/99) of the SWORs contained the hAT insertion, indicating a strong correlation between specific TE insertions in <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> and ecotype classification (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig6">6b</a>). The above TE insertions were further confirmed by PCR amplification and sequencing using transposon-specific primers (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">50</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">35</a>). We then analysed the haplotypes of six SNPs and the three TEs located within the 5.0-kb upstream and downstream regions and the coding sequence of <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig6">6c</a>). The results showed that the haplotypes of the three TEs were more consistent with ecotype information and flowering time than the haplotypes of the SNPs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">36</a>) and this result was also supported by PCA (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">34</a>), suggesting that these TE insertions in <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> could be used to roughly classify <i>B. napus</i> lines with unknown ecotype information into specific ecotypes, which would be very useful for rapeseed breeding.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Structural variations detected in BnaA10.FLC, BnaA02.FLC and BnaC02.FLC."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Structural variations detected in <i>BnaA10.FLC</i>, <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> and <i>BnaC02.FLC</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0577-7/MediaObjects/41477_2019_577_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0577-7/MediaObjects/41477_2019_577_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="312" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Insertions of four transposable elements around <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> in different ecotypes. <b>b</b>, Genotyping <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> in 141 <i>B. napus</i> accessions. The left were ecotypes of <i>B. napus</i> accessions. The middle is the read coverage of resequencing data in 15 representative sites, with Tapidor A10: 22,661,433–22,661,437; Westar A10: 23,731,730–23,731,734 and ZS11 A10: 23,942,298–23,942,302. The right is the PCR results statistics of three insertions. <b>c</b>, The haplotypes of six SNPs and three TEs around <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> in 141 <i>B. napus</i> accessions. <b>d</b>,<b>e</b>, SVs in <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> (<b>d</b>) and <i>BnaC02.FLC</i> (<b>e</b>) in eight accessions. <b>f</b>, The expression levels of <i>BnaA02.FLC</i>, <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> and <i>BnaC02.FLC</i> in plants before and after vernalization (T0 and T4) based on the number of fragments per kilobase of the exon model per million mapped reads (FPKM). ***<i>P</i> &lt; 0.001 (two-tailed Student’s <i>t</i>-test). Error bars indicate the mean ± s.d. (<i>n</i> = 2). ns, not spring; w, winter; s, spring; sw, semi-winter. <b>g</b>, The relationship between the accumulated days with low temperature, the cumulative expression levels of <i>FLCs</i>, <i>FTs</i> and flowering time in the 2018–2019 growing season in Wuhan. Three expressed <i>FT</i> genes were considered (average FPKM ≥ 1). Accumulated low-temperature curves indicated that the end of vernalization was in T2–T3 for SORs and T3–T4 for WORs. <b>h</b>, The cumulative expression levels of three <i>FLC</i> genes and the flowering time characterization of eight assembled <i>B. napus</i> accessions. Stacked histogram showed <i>FLCs</i> expression in T0–T3. These plants were transplanted from the field to the pot at 106 d after sowing. The standard deviation and average of flowering time were counted from 14–21 lines.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We further detected insertions in <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> on the basis of the resequencing data of 199 <i>B. rapa</i> accessions, including Chinese cabbage and paikoi<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Cheng, F. et al. Sub-genome parallel selection is associated with morphotype diversification and convergent crop domestication in Brassica rapa and Brassica oleracea. Nat. Genet. 48, 1218–1224 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2195">29</a></sup> and PCR amplification in another 192 oil-use and Chinese cabbage accessions. Of these <i>B. rapa</i> lines, 295 (75.4%) contained the 4,421-bp hAT insertion and 25 (6.4%) contained the MITE insertion, while 18.2% of the cabbage lines did not have either of these two insertions, suggesting that the insertion of these two TEs in <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> predated the formation of the allotetraploid <i>B. napus</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">37</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">51</a>). The SOR-specific LINE transposon was not found in the <i>B. rapa</i> accessions analysed in this study, which may be because the insertion of the LINE transposon occurred during the process of domestication and selection of <i>B. napus</i> or due to the limited number of <i>B. rapa</i> accessions analysed.</p><p>The 824-bp hAT inserted in the sixth exon of <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> was found in only two SORs and not in the other ecotypes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig6">6d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">38</a>). Functional <i>BnaC02.FLC</i> genes were found in the genomes of ZS11, Quinta and Westar, while this gene was replaced by <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> in the genomes of Shengli, Zheyou7 and Tapidor due to HE events (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">39</a>) or was completely deleted in the genomes of Gangan and No2127 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig6">6e</a>). Therefore, there are two copies of the <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> gene in Tapidor, Zheyou7 and Shengli, one copy of <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> and one copy of <i>BnaC02.FLC</i> in ZS11, Westar and Quinta, only one copy of <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> in Gangan and no <i>BnaC02.FLC</i> in No2127 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">52</a>).</p><p>In <i>B. napus</i>, late flowering and responsiveness to vernalization correlate with the level of <i>BnaFLC</i> messenger RNA (mRNA) transcripts. To further investigate the expression of the <i>BnaA10.FLC</i>, <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> and <i>BnaC02.FLC</i> genes in different ecotypes, we performed transcriptome sequencing using leaves at five stages (T0–T4) started from four-leaf seedlings at one-month intervals and analysed the expression levels of the <i>FLC</i> and <i>FT</i> genes before and after vernalization. The expression levels of <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> and <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> before vernalization (T0) displayed significant differences, which were associated with PAVs and copy number, among the eight accessions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig6">6f</a>). <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> was not expressed in two SORs (Westar and No2127) in any stage, and <i>BnaC02.FLC</i> was expressed in only Quinta, ZS11 and Westar. Unlike No2127, Westar has one functional <i>BnaC02.FLC</i>, which may explain the later flowering of Westar than of No2127. It has been reported that <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> has a stronger flowering repression effect than <i>BnaC02.FLC</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Tadege, M. et al. Control of flowering time by FLC orthologues in Brassica napus. Plant J. 28, 545–553 (2001)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2317">47</a></sup>. Although Tapidor and Quinta both possess <i>BnaA10.FLC</i>, Tapidor has two copies of <i>BnaA02.FLC</i>, while Quinta has one copy of <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> and one copy of <i>BnaC02.FLC</i>, which may cause the difference in flowering time between them (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig6">6h</a>). The expression levels of all three <i>FLCs</i> decreased after vernalization (T4). Simultaneously, the expression levels of the <i>FT</i> genes (<i>BnaA02.FT, BnaA07.FT</i> and <i>BnaC06.FT</i>) were negatively correlated with those of the <i>FLCs</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig6">6g</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">40</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">41</a>). The decrease in <i>FLC</i> transcripts perfectly coincided with the increase in <i>FT</i> transcripts (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig6">6g</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM1">41</a>), which also coincided with the flowering times of the eight accessions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig6">6h</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>In this study, we performed de novo assembly and annotation of eight representative <i>B. napus</i> genomes by integrating data from PacBio sequencing, Illumina paired-end short read sequencing and Hi-C technologies. Our assemblies are highly accurate and complete (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Tab1">1</a>) owing to the long-read sequencing technology and a scaffolding strategy based on chromatin interaction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2393">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Sun, F. et al. The high-quality genome of Brassica napus cultivar ‘ZS11’ reveals the introgression history in semi-winter morphotype. Plant J. 92, 452–468 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2396">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zou, J. et al. Genome-wide selection footprints and deleterious variations in young Asian allotetraploid rapeseed. Plant Biotechnol. J. 17, 1998–2010 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2399">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bayer, P. E. et al. Assembly and comparison of two closely related Brassica napus genomes. Plant Biotechnol. J. 15, 1602–1610 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2402">8</a></sup>, especially in regions enriched in TEs, such as centromere regions. In addition, this comprehensive comparative genomic analysis of eight <i>B. napus</i> genomes illustrates the power of multiple de novo assemblies in detecting genomic variations that have not been found by resequencing alone. Many intraspecific variations, including more than 16,720 PAVs, 1,360 inversions and 3,716 translocations and a series of SNPs and InDels, were revealed among the eight assemblies. We demonstrated that &gt;76% of the genes in the different genomes were located in syntenic regions and &gt;9.4% of genes were associated with large-effect mutations. Thus, these eight reference genomes represented the genetic information of three rapeseed ecotypes and provided useful resources for the identification of genomic variations in different rapeseed lines and for the understanding of genetic diversity and phenotypic heterogeneity.</p><p>Previous studies of crop pan-genomes confirmed that a single reference genome does not adequately represent the genetic diversity within a species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045–1052 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2412">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zhao, Q. et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat. Genet. 50, 278–284 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2415">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gan, X. et al. Multiple reference genomes and transcriptomes for Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 477, 419–423 (2011)." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2418">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, W. et al. Genomic variation in 3,010 diverse accessions of Asian cultivated rice. Nature 557, 43–49 (2018)." href="#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2418_1">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Montenegro, J. D. et al. The pangenome of hexaploid bread wheat. Plant J. 90, 1007–1013 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2421">52</a></sup>. Thus, we constructed a high-quality pan-genome of <i>B. napus</i> on the basis of the eight de novo assembled genomes and Darmor-<i>bzh</i>. The pan-genome reached ~1.8 Gb with 105,672 gene clusters that consisted of ~56% core-gene clusters, ~42% dispensable gene clusters and ~2% specific gene clusters. These results explain why several candidate genes associated with important agronomic traits in <i>B. napus</i> cannot be obtained directly by the homologous gene cloning approach from the Darmor-<i>bzh</i> reference genome (the fragment with the candidate genes was not present in the genome) but required the strategy of traditional BAC library construction and NGS sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Xia, S. et al. Altered transcription and neofunctionalization of duplicated genes rescue the harmful effects of a chimeric gene in Brassica napus. Plant Cell 28, 2060–2078 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2438">53</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Xin, Q. et al. MS5 mediates early meiotic progression and its natural variants may have applications for hybrid production in Brassica napus. Plant Cell 28, 1263–1278 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2441">54</a></sup>. The above results also indicate that the construction of the pan-genome is valuable for the study of important functional genes and genetic breeding of <i>B. napus</i>. To allow researchers to better use the pan-genome, we constructed a bioinformatics platform to reveal these data, which should greatly accelerate genetic studies and molecular breeding efforts, such as gene mapping, gene cloning, GWAS and marker-assisted selection, in oilseed rape.</p><p>Abundant evidence from genetics and molecular biology has clearly demonstrated that SVs can cause major phenotypic variance affecting a series of important agronomic and quality traits in crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Li, Y. H. et al. De novo assembly of soybean wild relatives for pan-genome analysis of diversity and agronomic traits. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1045–1052 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2451">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Tao, Y., Zhao, X., Mace, E., Henry, R. &amp; Jordan, D. Exploring and exploiting pan-genomics for crop improvement. Mol. Plant 12, 156–169 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2454">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zhao, Q. et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat. Genet. 50, 278–284 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2457">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Gabur, I. et al. Connecting genome structural variation with complex traits in crop plants. Theor. Appl. Genet. 132, 733–750 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2460">55</a></sup> and the characterization of SVs in populations is becoming the frontier of plant genomics. We used PAV-GWAS as a new strategy for screening candidate genes for traits. This technique yielded fewer significant associations than traditional SNP-GWAS but could focus on absent loci in the reference genome and obtain accurate positions. Subsequent analysis also showed that the PAVs among different ecotypes of <i>B. napus</i> directly affected the expression of the <i>FLC</i> genes and were involved in flowering regulation. Although many SNPs significantly associated with flowering time in <i>B. napus</i> were identified on both sides of the <i>FLC</i> genes of A02, A10 and C02 in previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zou, J. et al. Genome-wide selection footprints and deleterious variations in young Asian allotetraploid rapeseed. Plant Biotechnol. J. 17, 1998–2010 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2477">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Zhao, Q. et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat. Genet. 50, 278–284 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2480">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Gabur, I. et al. Connecting genome structural variation with complex traits in crop plants. Theor. Appl. Genet. 132, 733–750 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2483">55</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Xu, L. et al. Genome-wide association study reveals the genetic architecture of flowering time in rapeseed (Brassica napus L.). DNA Res. 23, 43–52 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2486">56</a></sup>, such SNPs are often proxies and not the functional variants driving the allelic difference in the <i>FLC</i> genes. These SNPs, rather than SVs, in these three <i>FLC</i> genes were also identified by sequence capture in four rapeseed accessions with different winter hardiness and vernalization requirements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Schiessl, S. et al. Capturing sequence variation among flowering-time regulatory gene homologs in the allopolyploid crop species Brassica napus. Front. Plant Sci. 5, 404 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2496">57</a></sup>. These results further validate that, compared to SNPs, SVs contribute more strongly to phenotype and the resulting phenotypic consequences.</p><p>We identified different types of SVs that altered the expression levels of three <i>FLC</i> genes. We can infer that <i>BnaA10.FLC</i>, <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> and <i>BnaC02.FLC</i> all participated in the vernalization response but <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> was the master regulator causing the differentiation of the three ecotypes of <i>B. napus</i>. Oilseed rape starts to flower when the low-temperature days exceed a critical number and the expression of three <i>BnaFLCs</i> and four <i>BnaFTs</i> achieve a threshold value (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#Fig6">6g</a>). Due to the LINE insertion in the first exon of <i>BnaA10.FLC</i>, the loss-of-function mutation makes SORs require weak or no vernalization. The MITE insertion in the promoter region of <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> enhances the expression of <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> which leads to a requirement of strong vernalization for WORs. A demand for vernalization of SWOR is somewhere between the other two ecotypes due to the hAT insertion in the promoter region of <i>BnaA10.FLC</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Plant materials</h3><p>Eight rapeseed accessions of three ecotypes named ZS11, Gangan, Zheyou7, Shengli, Tapidor, Quinta, Westar and No2127 were used in this study. ZS11 and Tapidor are the same as the two cultivars previous sequenced<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Sun, F. et al. The high-quality genome of Brassica napus cultivar ‘ZS11’ reveals the introgression history in semi-winter morphotype. Plant J. 92, 452–468 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2556">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bayer, P. E. et al. Assembly and comparison of two closely related Brassica napus genomes. Plant Biotechnol. J. 15, 1602–1610 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2559">8</a></sup>. Tapidor and Quinta are two typical WORs from Europe. Westar is an SOR from Canada that is widely used as transgenic receptor. No2127 is an artificially synthesized yellow-seeded SOR derived from hybridization between <i>B. rapa</i> and <i>B. oleracea</i> in Europe in the 1980s<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Chen, B. Y., Heneen, W. K. &amp; Jonsson, R. Resynthesis of Brassica napus L. through interspecific hybridization between B. alboglabra Bailey and B. campestris L. with special emphasis on seed colour. Plant Breed. 101, 52–59 (1988)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2569">15</a></sup>. Zheyou7 and ZS11 are two elite open-pollinated cultivars released in the 1970s and 2000s in China once or being widely cultivated in the provinces along the Yangtze River. Shengli is the first widely cultivated oilseed rape cultivar in China introduced from Japan in the 1950s. ZS11, Zheyou7, Gangan and Shengli are SWORs. All eight accessions were planted in the experimental field at Huazhong Agricultural University in Wuhan or in a greenhouse under a photoperiod of 16 h of light and 8 h of dark at 22 °C and 70% relative humidity. A natural population including 210 rapeseed accessions was collected from the world major rapeseed-growing countries to represent the genetic diversity of rapeseed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wang, B. et al. Dissection of the genetic architecture of three seed-quality traits and consequences for breeding in Brassica napus. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1336–1348 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2573">1</a></sup>. This natural population was planted in the experimental field in two SOR cultivation areas (Xining, Qinghai, 36° 35′ N, 101° 47′ E and Lanzhou, Gansu, 36° 02′ N, 103° 50′ E) in Northwest China in the 2013 and 2014 growing seasons and one SWOR cultivation area (Wuhan, 30° 36′ N, 104° 18′ E, China) in the 2014–2015 growing season. Flowering time was investigated and recorded as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Hou, J. et al. A Tourist-like MITE insertion in the upstream region of the BnFLC. A10 gene is associated with vernalization requirement in rapeseed (Brassica napus L.). BMC Plant Biol. 12, 238 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2578">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Long, Y. et al. Flowering time quantitative trait loci analysis of oilseed Brassica in multiple environments and genomewide alignment with Arabidopsis. Genetics 177, 2433–2444 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2581">58</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Illumina sequencing, PacBio library construction, sequencing and optical genome maps construction</h3><p>Illumina sequencing, PacBio sequencing and optical genome maps construction were performed at Novogene, China. High molecular weight DNA was extracted from 3-week-old seedlings. DNA fragments larger than 20 kb were selected by BluePippin electrophoresis (Sage Sciences). SMRTbell libraries were constructed as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Pendleton, M. et al. Assembly and diploid architecture of an individual human genome via single-molecule technologies. Nat. Methods 12, 780 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2593">59</a></sup> and sequenced on the PacBio Sequel platform (Pacific Biosciences). Illumina paired-end sequencing libraries were generated following manufacturer’s standard protocol (Illumina) and sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq platform. Paired-end 150-bp reads were generated from libraries with an insert size of 350 bp. Illumina genomic reads of each accession were used as the input of the Jellyfish tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Marçais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764–770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2597">60</a></sup> to obtain the <i>k</i>-mer frequency for estimating genome size. BioNano optical mapping was performed by digesting and labelling medium molecular weight DNA with the single-stranded nicking endonuclease Nt.BspQI according to BioNano’s standard protocol. The labelled DNA molecules were stretched and imaged with the BioNano Irys system (<a href="https://bionanogenomics.com/support-page/bionano-solve/">https://bionanogenomics.com/support-page/bionano-solve/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Hi-C library construction and sequencing</h3><p>About 1.5 g of 3-week-old seedlings were used for Hi-C experiment. The experiment procedures were similar to a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Xie, T. et al. De novo plant genome assembly based on chromatin interactions: a case study of Arabidopsis thaliana. Mol. Plant 8, 489–492 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2616">61</a></sup> but some steps were improved for efficiency. To digest extra protein and make the nuclei more permeable, the nuclei were resuspended in 150 μl of 0.5% SDS buffer and incubated at 62 °C for 5 min. Chromatin was digested for 12 h with 20 units of DpnII restriction enzyme (NEB) at 37 °C and the resuspended mixture was incubated at 62 °C for 20 min to inactivate the restriction digestion. The DNA pieces between 300 and 500 bp were excised and purified using Ampure XP beads (Beckman Coulter). The library was constructed by an Illumina TruSeq DNA Sample Prep Kit and sequenced by Illumina Hiseq Xten with 2 × 150-bp reads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Contig assembly and polishing</h3><p>De novo genome assembly was performed mainly using the PacBio SMRT long reads. Subreads polishing and contigs assembly was primarily carried out using Falcon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2628">62</a></sup> (falcon-2017.11.02–16.04-py2.7) with length_cutoff_pr = 6,000. We additionally configured pa_HPCdaligner_option = -v -B128 -t32 -e.75 -h480 -l3200 -w8 -T8, ovlp_HPCdaligner_option = -v -B128 -t32 -e.96 -l2500 -T8, falcon_sense_option = –output_multi–min_idt 0.70–min_cov 3–max_n_read 300, overlap_filtering_setting = –max_diff 110–max_cov 165–min_cov 3–bestn 10 with parameters optimized for eight <i>B. napus</i> genomes assembly. The subreads were assembled using Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2635">63</a></sup> v.1.6 after Falcon polishing with correctedErrorRate = 0.05. We mapped PacBio sequencing reads to the draft contigs acquired by Canu and Falcon using pbalign and polished the resulting contigs using Quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2639">64</a></sup> with arrow as algorithm. On this basis, contigs were polished using Illumina PE reads (insertion size = 350 bp) and pilon 1.18 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2643">65</a></sup>). For the polished contigs, the unique sequences in Canu assembly while not being contained in Falcon assembly were merged to obtain final contigs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Pseudo-chromosome construction</h3><p>Pseudo-chromosome was constructed with Hi-C data using the 3D-DNA pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Dudchenko, O. et al. De novo assembly of the Aedes aegypti genome using Hi-C yields chromosome-length scaffolds. Science 356, 92–95 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2656">66</a></sup>. The Hi-C reads were aligned to the polished contigs using the Juicer pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicer provides a one-click system for analyzing loop-resolution Hi-C experiments. Cell Syst. 3, 95–98 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2660">67</a></sup>. The 3D-DNA pipeline was run with the following parameters: -i 1 -r 5. The results were polished using the Juicebox Assembly Tools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicebox provides a visualization system for Hi-C contact maps with unlimited zoom. Cell Syst. 3, 99–101 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2664">68</a></sup>. The Hi-C scaffolding resulted in 19 chromosome-length scaffolds. The scaffolds nomenclature was adopted for the chromosome numbering on the basis of their collinearity with 19 chromosomes of Darmor-<i>bzh</i> genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>Protein sequences of <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>B. oleracea</i>, <i>B. rapa</i> and eight assembled <i>B. napus</i> accessions were compared using BLASTP (e-value cut-off 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>). Orthologous groups of sequences were constructed on the basis of the best bidirectional hits. A total of 1,235 groups with single member from each species were selected and the sequences of each organism were concatenated into one long protein sequence. Concatenated sequences were aligned using MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Katoh, K. et al. MAFFT version 5: improvement in accuracy of multiple sequence alignment. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 511–518 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2694">69</a></sup> and well-aligned regions were extracted using Gblocks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Castresana, J. Selection of conserved blocks from multiple alignments for their use in phylogenetic analysis. Mol. Biol. Evol. 17, 540–552 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2698">70</a></sup> with −t = p, −b4 = 5, −b5 = h. Trees were then constructed using multithreaded RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2702">71</a></sup>, the PROTGAMMAWAG model and 100 bootstrap replicates. Tag SNPs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Carlson, C. S. et al. Selecting a maximally informative set of single-nucleotide polymorphisms for association analyses using linkage disequilibrium. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 74, 106–120 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2706">72</a></sup> were selected using PLINK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Purcell, S. et al. PLINK: a tool set for whole-genome association and population-based linkage analyses. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 81, 559–575 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2710">73</a></sup> (v.1.90) with parameter ‘–blocks’ to construct neighbour-joining tree. The neighbour-joining tree was constructed using TreeBeST (v.1.9.2, <a href="https://github.com/lh3/treebest">https://github.com/lh3/treebest</a>) software with 1,000 replicates of bootstrap. An online tool Interactive tree of life (iTOL)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Letunic, I. &amp; Bork, P. Interactive tree of life (iTOL) v3: an online tool for the display and annotation of phylogenetic and other trees. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, W242–W245 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2719">74</a></sup> v.3 was used to display the neighbour-joining tree. PCA of all SNPs were performed using genome-wide complex trait analysis (GCTA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Yang, J., Lee, S. H., Goddard, M. E. &amp; Visscher, P. M. GCTA: a tool for genome-wide complex trait analysis. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 88, 76–82 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2723">75</a></sup> v.1.91.7 software with default parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">SNPs and InDels analysis of different accessions</h3><p>The remaining seven genomes were aligned to reference genome ZS11 using Mummer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Delcher, A. L., Salzberg, S. L. &amp; Phillippy, A. M. Using MUMmer to identify similar regions in large sequence sets. Curr. Protoc. Bioinforma. 0, 10.3.1–10.3.18 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2735">76</a></sup> (v.3.23) with parameters settings ‘-g 1000 -c 90 -l 40’. The alignment block was then filtered out of the mapping noise and the one-to-one alignment was identified by delta-filter with parameters settings ‘-r -q’. Show-snps was used to identify SNPs and InDels (&lt;100 bp) with parameter setting ‘-ClrTH’. All clean reads were mapped to the ZS11 genome using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows-Wheeler Transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2739">77</a></sup> with the default parameters. Picard program was used to filter the PCR duplicates of reads and reads around InDels were realigned with the IndelRealigner option in the genome analysis toolkit (GATK)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2743">78</a></sup>. The unique mapping data were used for identifying SNPs using the GATK. Only variations detected with both tools were identified as high-quality SNPs. The distribution of SNPs in the genome was demonstrated by Circos<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Krzywinski, M. et al. Circos: an information aesthetic for comparative genomics. Genome Res. 19, 1639–1645 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2747">79</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Structural variation analysis of different accessions</h3><p>To identify translocations and inversions, we aligned the other seven genomes to the ZS11 reference genome assembled in this study using Mummer. For the original alignment block to be filtered, we picked a unique alignment block that is longer than 1,000 bp. Aligned blocks that appeared as reverse matches as potential inversion regions were further manually checked and merged. At the same time, the alignment block between different chromosomes was considered to be an interchromosomal translocation. SyRI (<a href="https://github.com/schneebergerlab/syri">https://github.com/schneebergerlab/syri</a>) was used to identify translocation regions on the basis of the presence of non-collinear alignment blocks on both sides. We used the same method as Sun et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Sun, S. et al. Extensive intraspecific gene order and gene structural variations between Mo17 and other maize genomes. Nat. Genet. 50, 1289–1295 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2763">80</a></sup> to identify genes with large structure variations, which mapped gene sequence (extending the longest transcript of each gene 2 kb upstream and downstream) to query genomes using BWA-MEM.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Identification of PAVs and pan-reference genome construction</h3><p>The potential PAV sequences of seven genomes relative to reference genome ZS11 were identified using show-diff in Mummer (v.3.23). First, sequences that intersected with the gap region in the respective genome were excluded. On the other hand, sequence with feature type ‘BRK’ was filtered out, which was considered to be non-reference sequence which aligned to the gap-start or gap-end bounder. To identify the true respective unique sequences, the candidate PAV sequence was mapped to the ZS11 genome with parameter setting ‘-x asm10’ using minimap2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Li, H. Minimap2: pairwise alignment for nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 34, 3094–3100 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2775">81</a></sup>) and the sequence covering &gt;80% was filtered out to obtain the final PAV region. The gene having &gt;80% overlap with PAV region was considered to be a PAV-related gene. Further, we used BWA-MEM to align Illumina reads of ZS11 to seven genomes to rule out the effects of false positives and filtered out genes covering &gt;50% of the genes to obtain the final PAV genes. We stepwise added the PAV sequence and PAV genes with the order ZS11, Gangan, Zheyou7, Shengli, Tapidor, Westar, No2127 and Darmor to the current genome to construct a pan-reference genome.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Gene index in nine <i>B. napus</i>
</h3><p>MCscanx<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2791">82</a></sup> was used to identify collinear orthologues between query genomes and ZS11 genome, with at least five homologous genes and fewer than ten gaps required to call a collinear block. On the other hand, some translocations would also not need to meet the threshold for syntonic search. Therefore, for the genes that have established gene index pairs with ZS11 or with at least one species, we also added reciprocal best-hits as evidence to construct gene index pairs. We combined all collinear orthologues to construct the <i>B. napus</i> gene index with a set of unique ID (HUBna).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">SNP genotyping and SNP-GWAS of BN-NAM population</h3><p>The 15 RIL families of BN-NAM population were genotyped by sequencing previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Hu, J. et al. Genetic properties of a nested association mapping population constructed with semi-winter and spring oilseed rapes. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 1740 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2807">41</a></sup>. The genotypes of all 15 RIL families in the BN-NAM population were reanalysed according to the original method as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Hu, J. et al. Genetic properties of a nested association mapping population constructed with semi-winter and spring oilseed rapes. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 1740 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2811">41</a></sup> but using the newly assembled ZS11 genome sequence as the reference. A joint linkage map containing 122,899 SNPs was generated by integrating SNPs in the 15 RIL families. A set of 5,419,567 SNPs were identified for the whole-genome variant map according to the original method, as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Hu, J. et al. Genetic properties of a nested association mapping population constructed with semi-winter and spring oilseed rapes. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 1740 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2815">41</a></sup>. GWAS was performed using a mixed linear model (MLM) in genome-wide efficient mixed model association (GEMMA) software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Zhou, X. &amp; Stephens, M. Genome-wide efficient mixed-model analysis for association studies. Nat. Genet. 44, 821–824 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2819">83</a></sup>. MLM was coupled with estimated relatedness matrix as a random effect, which was estimated by GEMMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Zhou, X. &amp; Stephens, M. Genome-wide efficient mixed-model analysis for association studies. Nat. Genet. 44, 821–824 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2823">83</a></sup>. The effective number of independent markers (<i>N</i>) was calculated using the GEC tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Li, M. X. et al. Evaluating the effective numbers of independent tests and significant p-value thresholds in commercial genotyping arrays and public imputation reference datasets. Hum. Genet. 131, 747–756 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2831">84</a></sup> and the suggestive <i>P</i> value (1/<i>N</i>) was set as the threshold<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Yang, W. et al. Combining high-throughput phenotyping and genome-wide association studies to reveal natural genetic variation in rice. Nat. Commun. 5, 5087 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2841">85</a></sup>. To identify independent peak SNPs of association signals, SNPs passing the <i>P</i> value of GWAS thresholds were further clumped to remove the dependent makers caused by linkage disequilibrium (LD; <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> &gt; 0.30) and interval (±500 kb) using the clumping function in Plink. <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> represents the degree of linkage disequilibrium.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">PAV genotyping and PAV-GWAS</h3><p>To construct PAV haplotype maps of the 16 founder accessions of the NAM population, we combined PAVs across eight <i>B. napus</i> accessions identified by whole-genome comparative analysis using the ‘bayesTyperTools combine’ module in BayesTyper v.1.3.1 and the vcf file contained 88,781 AVs and 95,772 PVs as variant candidates in 19 pseudo-chromosomes. The WGS reads <i>k</i>-mers of 16 founder accessions of the NAM population were counted using KMC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Kokot, M., Długosz, M. &amp; Deorowicz, S. KMC 3: counting and manipulating k-mer statistics. Bioinformatics 33, 2759–2761 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2871">86</a></sup> v.3.1.0 with parameters –k55 and –ci1. A read <i>k</i>-mer bloom filter was created from the KMC output using the ‘bayesTyperTools makeBloom’ module with default parameters. The ‘bayesTyper genotype’ module was used to estimate genotypes of each line based on <i>k</i>-mer counts from sequencing reads. To accurately estimate the noise parameters, the genotyping procedure included all the PAVs and 1 × 10<sup>6</sup> random SNVs; all the unplaced contigs in the reference genome were treated as decoy. Then, the PAV haplotype maps of parent lines were mapped to 2,141 RILs based on high-density genetic linkage map as previously described in SNPs genotyping<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Hu, J. et al. Genetic properties of a nested association mapping population constructed with semi-winter and spring oilseed rapes. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 1740 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2884">41</a></sup>. Finally, 27,216 unique polymorphic PAVs were obtained with MAF large than 0.05. PAV-GWAS was performed for silique length, seed weight and flowering time using MLM in GEMMA v.0.98 software.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Population structure and genotype analysis of <i>FLC</i>
</h3><p>To obtain the genotypes of <i>Bna.A02FLC</i> and <i>Bna.A10FLC</i> in the natural population consisting of 210 rapeseed accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wang, B. et al. Dissection of the genetic architecture of three seed-quality traits and consequences for breeding in Brassica napus. Plant Biotechnol. J. 16, 1336–1348 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2906">1</a></sup>, we mapped the resequencing data to the genomes of ZS11, Westar and Tapidor, representing the SWOR, SOR and WOR ecotypes, respectively, using BWA-MEM. SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Etherington, G. J., Ramirez-Gonzalez, R. H. &amp; MacLean, D. bio-samtools 2: a package for analysis and visualization of sequence and alignment data with SAMtools in Ruby. Bioinformatics 31, 2565–2567 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d7326e2910">87</a></sup> was used to filter uniquely mapped reads with parameter setting ‘-q 30’. Normalized coverage was defined as the ratio of the insertion site coverage and the average coverage of 100 kb of surrounding genes. Searching for unique fragments on both sides of insert fragment, 30 candidate loci were selected among three genomes. The R PCA function was used to perform PCA to screen out the representative points that contributed to the interpretation of the data variation. Each PAV in <i>BnaA10.FLC</i> and <i>BnaA02.FLC</i> was genotyped on the basis of the coverage of 15 and five representative sites, respectively. Normalized coverage higher than 0.25 was considered to cover the insertion fragment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0577-7#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>All the raw sequencing data generated during the current study are available in the NCBI BioProject under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/PRJNA546246">PRJNA546246</a>. The genome assemblies and annotation files are available at the website <a href="http://cbi.hzau.edu.cn/bnapus">http://cbi.hzau.edu.cn/bnapus</a>. All the materials in this study, including introgression lines, are available upon request.</p>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;dopt=Abstract&amp;list_uids=17453172" aria-label="View reference 6 on PubMed" rel="nofollow">PubMed</a>
